            </li>

       <tr>
        <td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">
         <div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
          <ul>
           <li class="nv-view">
            <a href="/wiki/Template:Indo-European_topics" title="Template:Indo-European topics">
             <abbr title="View this template">
              v
             </abbr>
            </a>
           </li>
           <li class="nv-talk">
            <a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Indo-European_topics" title="Template talk:Indo-European topics">
             <abbr title="Discuss this template">
              t
             </abbr>
            </a>
           </li>
           <li class="nv-edit">
            <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Indo-European_topics&amp;action=edit">
             <abbr title="Edit this template">
              e
             </abbr>
            </a>
           </li>
          </ul>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       The
       <b>
        Celtic languages
       </b>
       (usually
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pronunciation_of_Celtic" title="Pronunciation of Celtic">
        pronounced
       </a>
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt">
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">
          /
          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
           <span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">
            ˈ
           </span>
           <span title="'k' in 'kind'">
            k
           </span>
           <span title="/ɛ/: 'e' in 'dress'">
            ɛ
           </span>
           <span title="'l' in 'lie'">
            l
           </span>
           <span title="'t' in 'tie'">
            t
           </span>
           <span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">
            ɪ
           </span>
           <span title="'k' in 'kind'">
            k
           </span>
          </span>
          /
         </a>
        </span>
       </span>
       but sometimes
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt">
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">
          /
          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
           <span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">
            ˈ
           </span>
           <span title="'s' in 'sigh'">
            s
           </span>
           <span title="/ɛ/: 'e' in 'dress'">
            ɛ
           </span>
           <span title="'l' in 'lie'">
            l
           </span>
           <span title="'t' in 'tie'">
            t
           </span>
           <span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">
            ɪ
           </span>
           <span title="'k' in 'kind'">
            k
           </span>
          </span>
          /
         </a>
        </span>
       </span>
       )
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       are descended from
       <a href="/wiki/Proto-Celtic_language" title="Proto-Celtic language">
        Proto-Celtic
       </a>
       , or "Common Celtic"; a branch of the greater
       <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">
        Indo-European
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Language_family" title="Language family">
        language family
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The term "Celtic" was first used to describe this language group by
       <a href="/wiki/Edward_Lhuyd" title="Edward Lhuyd">
        Edward Lhuyd
       </a>
       in 1707,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       following
       <a href="/wiki/Paul-Yves_Pezron" title="Paul-Yves Pezron">
        Paul-Yves Pezron
       </a>
       who had already made the explicit link between the Celts described by classical writers and the
       <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">
        Welsh
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Breton_language" title="Breton language">
        Breton
       </a>
       languages.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Modern Celtic languages are mostly spoken on the north-western edge of
       <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">
        Europe
       </a>
       , notably in
       <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">
        Ireland
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">
        Scotland
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">
        Wales
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Brittany" title="Brittany">
        Brittany
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">
        Cornwall
       </a>
       , and the
       <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">
        Isle of Man
       </a>
       . There are also a substantial number of
       <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">
        Welsh
       </a>
       speakers in the
       <a href="/wiki/Patagonia" title="Patagonia">
        Patagonia
       </a>
       area of
       <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">
        Argentina
       </a>
       and some speakers of
       <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic" title="Scottish Gaelic">
        Scottish Gaelic
       </a>
       on
       <a href="/wiki/Cape_Breton_Island" title="Cape Breton Island">
        Cape Breton Island
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Nova_Scotia" title="Nova Scotia">
        Nova Scotia
       </a>
       . Some people speak Celtic languages in the other
       <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Celtic_diaspora_(disambiguation)" title="Celtic diaspora (disambiguation)">
        Celtic diaspora
       </a>
       areas of the United States,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mla_6-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-mla-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Canada, Australia,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and New Zealand.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In all these areas, the Celtic languages are now only spoken by minorities though there are continuing efforts at
       <a href="/wiki/Language_revitalization" title="Language revitalization">
        revitalisation
       </a>
       . Welsh is the only Celtic language not classified as "endangered" by
       <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">
        UNESCO
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       During the 1st millennium BC, they were spoken across much of Europe, in the
       <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">
        Iberian Peninsula
       </a>
       , from the Atlantic and
       <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">
        North Sea
       </a>
       coastlines, up to the
       <a href="/wiki/Rhine" title="Rhine">
        Rhine
       </a>
       valley and down the
       <a href="/wiki/Danube" title="Danube">
        Danube
       </a>
       valley to the
       <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">
        Black Sea
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">
        northern Balkan Peninsula
       </a>
       and in central
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">
        Asia Minor
       </a>
       . The spread to Cape Breton and Patagonia occurred in modern times.
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Living_languages">
        Living languages
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Celtic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Living languages">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">

      </h2>
      <p>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SIL_Ethnologue" title="SIL Ethnologue">
        SIL Ethnologue
       </a>
       lists six living Celtic languages, of which four have retained a substantial number of native speakers. These are the
       <a href="/wiki/Goidelic_languages" title="Goidelic languages">
        Goidelic languages
       </a>
       (i.e.
       <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">
        Irish
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic" title="Scottish Gaelic">
        Scottish Gaelic
       </a>
       , which are both descended from
       <a href="/wiki/Middle_Irish" title="Middle Irish">
        Middle Irish
       </a>
       ) and the
       <a href="/wiki/Brittonic_languages" title="Brittonic languages">
        Brittonic languages
       </a>
       (i.e.
       <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">
        Welsh
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Breton_language" title="Breton language">
        Breton
       </a>
       , which are both descended from
       <a href="/wiki/Common_Brittonic" title="Common Brittonic">
        Common Brittonic
       </a>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The other two,
       <a href="/wiki/Cornish_language" title="Cornish language">
        Cornish
       </a>
       (a Brittonic language) and
       <a href="/wiki/Manx_language" title="Manx language">
        Manx
       </a>
       (a Goidelic language), died in modern times
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       with their presumed last native speakers in
       <a href="/wiki/Last_speaker_of_the_Cornish_language" title="Last speaker of the Cornish language">
        1777
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Ned_Maddrell" title="Ned Maddrell">
        1974
       </a>
       respectively. For both these languages, however,
       <a href="/wiki/Language_revitalization" title="Language revitalization">
        revitalisation
       </a>
       movements have led to the adoption of these languages by adults and children and produced some native speakers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iomtoday.co.im_13-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-iomtoday.co.im-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Taken together, there were roughly one million native speakers of Celtic languages as of the 2000s.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 2010, there were more than 1.4 million speakers of Celtic languages.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>

      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Mixed_languages">
        Mixed languages
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Celtic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Mixed languages">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Shelta" title="Shelta">
         Shelta
        </a>
        , based largely on
        <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">
         Irish
        </a>
        with influence from an undocumented source (some 86,000 speakers in 2009).
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
         <a href="#cite_note-44">
          [44]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        Some forms of
        <a href="/wiki/Welsh-Romani_language" title="Welsh-Romani language">
         Welsh-Romani
        </a>
        or Kååle also combined Romany itself with
        <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">
         Welsh language
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">
         English language
        </a>
        forms (extinct).
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
         <a href="#cite_note-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beurla-reagaird" title="Beurla-reagaird">
         Beurla-reagaird
        </a>
        , Highland travellers' language
       </li>
      </ul>

       <span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">
        Classification
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">

        </a>

         ]
        </span>
       </span>

         Classification of Indo-European languages.
         <i>
          (click to enlarge)
         </i>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Celtic is divided into various branches:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Lepontic_language" title="Lepontic language">
         Lepontic
        </a>
        , the oldest attested Celtic language (from the 6th century BC).
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schumacher_46-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Schumacher-46">
          [46]
         </a>
        </sup>
        Anciently spoken in
        <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">
         Switzerland
        </a>
        and in Northern-Central
        <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">
         Italy
        </a>
        , from the
        <a href="/wiki/Alps" title="Alps">
         Alps
        </a>
        to
        <a href="/wiki/Umbria" title="Umbria">
         Umbria
        </a>
        . Coins with Lepontic inscriptions have been found in
        <a href="/wiki/Noricum" title="Noricum">
         Noricum
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Gallia_Narbonensis" title="Gallia Narbonensis">
         Gallia Narbonensis
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
         <a href="#cite_note-47">
          [47]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kruta2_48-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-kruta2-48">
          [48]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
         <a href="#cite_note-49">
          [49]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
         <a href="#cite_note-50">
          [50]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bronce_de_Botorrita_II.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="679" data-file-width="1463" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Bronce_de_Botorrita_II.jpg/300px-Bronce_de_Botorrita_II.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Bronce_de_Botorrita_II.jpg/450px-Bronce_de_Botorrita_II.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Bronce_de_Botorrita_II.jpg/600px-Bronce_de_Botorrita_II.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bronce_de_Botorrita_II.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The second of the four
         <a href="/wiki/Botorrita_plaque" title="Botorrita plaque">
          Botorrita plaques
         </a>
         . The third plaque is the longest text discovered in any ancient Celtic language.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
          <a href="#cite_note-51">
           [51]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Celtiberian_language" title="Celtiberian language">
         Northeastern Hispano-Celtic
        </a>
        /
        <a href="/wiki/Celtiberian_language" title="Celtiberian language">
         Eastern Hispano-Celtic
        </a>
        or
        <a href="/wiki/Celtiberian_language" title="Celtiberian language">
         Celtiberian
        </a>
        , anciently spoken in the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iberian_peninsula" title="Iberian peninsula">
         Iberian peninsula
        </a>
        ,
        <div class="thumb tright">
         <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Greek_and_Phoenician_Colonies_in_The_Iberian_Peninsula.png">
           <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1659" data-file-width="2025" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Greek_and_Phoenician_Colonies_in_The_Iberian_Peninsula.png/220px-Greek_and_Phoenician_Colonies_in_The_Iberian_Peninsula.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Greek_and_Phoenician_Colonies_in_The_Iberian_Peninsula.png/330px-Greek_and_Phoenician_Colonies_in_The_Iberian_Peninsula.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Greek_and_Phoenician_Colonies_in_The_Iberian_Peninsula.png/440px-Greek_and_Phoenician_Colonies_in_The_Iberian_Peninsula.png 2x" width="220"/>
          </a>
          <div class="thumbcaption">
           <div class="magnify">
            <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Greek_and_Phoenician_Colonies_in_The_Iberian_Peninsula.png" title="Enlarge">
            </a>
           </div>
           Pre-Roman map of The Iberian Peninsula
          </div>
         </div>
        </div>
        in the eastern part of
        <a href="/wiki/Old_Castile" title="Old Castile">
         Old Castile
        </a>
        and south of
        <a href="/wiki/Aragon" title="Aragon">
         Aragon
        </a>
        . Modern provinces of Segovia, Burgos, Soria, Guadalajara, Cuenca, Zaragoza and Teruel. The relationship of Celtiberian with
        <a href="/wiki/Gallaecian_language" title="Gallaecian language">
         Gallaecian
        </a>
        , in the northwest of the peninsula, is uncertain.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
         <a href="#cite_note-52">
          [52]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
         <a href="#cite_note-53">
          [53]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Gallaecian_language" title="Gallaecian language">
         Northwestern Hispano-Celtic
        </a>
        /
        <a href="/wiki/Gallaecian_language" title="Gallaecian language">
         Western Hispano-Celtic
        </a>
        , anciently spoken in the northwest of the peninsula (modern
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_Portugal" title="Northern Portugal">
         northern Portugal
        </a>
        ,
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galicia,_Spain" title="Galicia, Spain">
         Galicia
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Asturias" title="Asturias">
         Asturias
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Cantabria" title="Cantabria">
         Cantabria
        </a>
        and parts of modern
        <a href="/wiki/Old_Castile" title="Old Castile">
         Old Castile
        </a>
        ).
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
         <a href="#cite_note-54">
          [54]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Gaulish_language" title="Gaulish language">
         Gaulish languages
        </a>
        , including
        <a href="/wiki/Galatian_language" title="Galatian language">
         Galatian
        </a>
        and possibly
        <a href="/wiki/Noric_language" title="Noric language">
         Noric
        </a>
        . These languages were once spoken in a wide arc from
        <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">
         Belgium
        </a>
        to
        <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">
         Turkey
        </a>
        . They are now all extinct.
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Brittonic_languages" title="Brittonic languages">
         Brittonic
        </a>
        , including the living languages
        <a href="/wiki/Breton_language" title="Breton language">
         Breton
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Cornish_language" title="Cornish language">
         Cornish
        </a>
        , and
        <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">
         Welsh
        </a>
        , and the extinct languages
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cumbric_language" title="Cumbric language">
         Cumbric
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Pictish_language" title="Pictish language">
         Pictish
        </a>
        though Pictish may be a sister language rather than a daughter of
        <a href="/wiki/Common_Brittonic" title="Common Brittonic">
         Common Brittonic
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
         <a href="#cite_note-55">
          [55]
         </a>
        </sup>
        Before the arrival of Scotti on the Isle of Man in the 9th century, there may have been a Brittonic language in the Isle of Man.
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Goidelic_languages" title="Goidelic languages">
         Goidelic
        </a>
        , including the living languages
        <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">
         Irish
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Manx_language" title="Manx language">
         Manx
        </a>
        , and
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic_language" title="Scottish Gaelic language">
         Scottish Gaelic
        </a>
        .
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Scholarly handling of the Celtic languages has been rather argumentative owing to scarceness of primary source data. Some scholars (such as Cowgill 1975; McCone 1991, 1992; and Schrijver 1995) distinguish
       <a href="/wiki/Continental_Celtic_languages" title="Continental Celtic languages">
        Continental Celtic
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Insular_Celtic_languages" title="Insular Celtic languages">
        Insular Celtic
       </a>
       , arguing that the differences between the Goidelic and Brittonic languages arose after these split off from the Continental Celtic languages.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
        <a href="#cite_note-56">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Other scholars (such as Schmidt 1988) distinguish between
       <i>
        P-Celtic
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        Q-Celtic
       </i>
       , putting most of the Gaulish and Brittonic languages in the former group and the Goidelic and Celtiberian languages in the latter. The P-Celtic languages (also called
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gallo-Brittonic" title="Gallo-Brittonic">
        Gallo-Brittonic
       </a>
       ) are sometimes seen (for example by Koch 1992) as a central innovating area as opposed to the more conservative peripheral Q-Celtic languages.
      </p>
      <p>
       The Breton language is Brittonic, not Gaulish, though there may be some input from the latter,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
        <a href="#cite_note-57">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
       having been introduced from Southwestern regions of Britain in the post-Roman era and having evolved into Breton – still partially intelligible by modern Welsh and Cornish speakers.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In the P/Q classification schema, the first language to split off from Proto-Celtic was Gaelic. It has characteristics that some scholars see as archaic, but others see as also being in the Brittonic languages (see Schmidt). In the Insular/Continental classification schema, the split of the former into Gaelic and Brittonic is seen as being late.
      </p>
      <p>
       The distinction of Celtic into these four sub-families most likely occurred about 900 BC according to Gray and Atkinson
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
        <a href="#cite_note-58">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
        <a href="#cite_note-59">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but, because of estimation uncertainty, it could be any time between 1200 and 800 BC. However, they only considered Gaelic and Brythonic. The controversial paper by Forster and Toth
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
        <a href="#cite_note-60">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
       included Gaulish and put the break-up much earlier at 3200 BC ± 1500 years. They support the Insular Celtic hypothesis. The early Celts were commonly associated with the archaeological
       <a href="/wiki/Urnfield_culture" title="Urnfield culture">
        Urnfield culture
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Hallstatt_culture" title="Hallstatt culture">
        Hallstatt culture
       </a>
       , and the
       <a href="/wiki/La_T%C3%A8ne_culture" title="La Tène culture">
        La Tène culture
       </a>
       , though the earlier assumption of association between language and culture is now considered to be less strong.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Renfrew_61-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Renfrew-61">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-James_62-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-James-62">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
       r
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:177px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Celtic_Nations-flag_shades.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1300" data-file-width="675" height="337" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Map_of_Celtic_Nations-flag_shades.svg/175px-Map_of_Celtic_Nations-flag_shades.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Map_of_Celtic_Nations-flag_shades.svg/263px-Map_of_Celtic_Nations-flag_shades.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Map_of_Celtic_Nations-flag_shades.svg/350px-Map_of_Celtic_Nations-flag_shades.svg.png 2x" width="175"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Celtic_Nations-flag_shades.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The
         <a href="/wiki/Celtic_nations" title="Celtic nations">
          Celtic nations
         </a>
         , where Celtic languages are spoken today, or were spoken into the modern era:
         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
          <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #009E60; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
          </span>
          <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">
           Ireland
          </a>
          (
          <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">
           Irish
          </a>
          )
         </div>
         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
          <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #0072C6; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
          </span>
          <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">
           Scotland
          </a>
          (
          <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic" title="Scottish Gaelic">
           Scottish Gaelic
          </a>
          )
         </div>
         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
          <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #D3B04A; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
          </span>
          <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">
           Isle of Man
          </a>
          (
          <a href="/wiki/Manx_language" title="Manx language">
           Manx
          </a>
          )
         </div>
         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
          <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:red; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
          </span>
          <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">
           Wales
          </a>
          (
          <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">
           Welsh
          </a>
          )
         </div>
         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
          <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #FFD700; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
          </span>
          <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">
           Cornwall
          </a>
          (
          <a href="/wiki/Cornish_language" title="Cornish language">
           Cornish
          </a>
          )
         </div>
         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
          <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:black; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
          </span>
          <a href="/wiki/Brittany" title="Brittany">
           Brittany
          </a>
          (
          <a href="/wiki/Breton_language" title="Breton language">
           Breton
          </a>
          )
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       There are legitimate scholarly arguments in favour of both the Insular Celtic hypothesis and the P-Celtic/Q-Celtic hypothesis. Proponents of each schema dispute the accuracy and usefulness of the other's categories. However, since the 1970s the division into Insular and Continental Celtic has become the more widely held view (Cowgill 1975; McCone 1991, 1992; Schrijver 1995), but in the middle of the 1980s, the P-Celtic/Q-Celtic hypothesis found new supporters (Lambert 1994), because of the inscription on the Larzac piece of lead (1983), the analysis of which reveals another common phonetical innovation
       <i>
        -nm-
       </i>
       &gt;
       <i>
        -nu
       </i>
       (Gaelic
       <i>
        ainm
       </i>
       / Gaulish
       <i>
        anuana
       </i>
       , Old Welsh
       <i>
        enuein
       </i>
       "names"), that is less accidental than only one. The discovery of a third common innovation, would allow the specialists to come to the conclusion of a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gallo-Brittonic" title="Gallo-Brittonic">
        Gallo-Brittonic
       </a>
       dialect (Schmidt 1986; Fleuriot 1986).
      </p>
      <p>
       The interpretation of this and further evidence is still quite contested, and the main argument in favour of Insular Celtic is connected with the development of the verbal morphology and the syntax in Irish and British Celtic, which Schumacher regards as convincing, while he considers the P-Celtic/Q-Celtic division unimportant and treats Gallo-Brittonic as an outdated hypothesis.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schumacher_46-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Schumacher-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Stifter affirms that the Gallo-Brittonic view is "out of favour" in the scholarly community as of 2008 and the Insular Celtic hypothesis "widely accepted".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
        <a href="#cite_note-63">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       When referring only to the modern Celtic languages, since no Continental Celtic language has living descendants, "Q-Celtic" is equivalent to "Goidelic" and "P-Celtic" is equivalent to "Brittonic".
      </p>
      <p>
       Within the
       <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">
        Indo-European
       </a>
       family, the Celtic languages have sometimes been placed with the
       <a href="/wiki/Italic_languages" title="Italic languages">
        Italic languages
       </a>
       in a common
       <a href="/wiki/Italo-Celtic" title="Italo-Celtic">
        Italo-Celtic
       </a>
       subfamily, a hypothesis that is now largely discarded, in favour of the assumption of
       <a href="/wiki/Language_contact" title="Language contact">
        language contact
       </a>
       between pre-Celtic and pre-Italic communities.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2014)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       How the family tree of the Celtic languages is ordered depends on which hypothesis is used:
      </p>
      <div>
       <table class="multicol" role="presentation" style="border-collapse: collapse; padding: 0; border: 0; background:transparent; width:40%;">
        <tr>
         <td style="width:50%; text-align:left; vertical-align:top;">
          <p>
           "
           <b>
            Insular Celtic hypothesis
           </b>
           "
          </p>
          <ul>
           <li>
            Proto-Celtic
            <ul>
             <li>
              <a href="/wiki/Continental_Celtic_languages" title="Continental Celtic languages">
               Continental Celtic
              </a>
              <ul>
               <li>
                <a href="/wiki/Celtiberian_language" title="Celtiberian language">
                 Celtiberian
                </a>
               </li>
               <li>
                <a href="/wiki/Gallaecian_language" title="Gallaecian language">
                 Gallaecian
                </a>
               </li>
               <li>
                <a href="/wiki/Gaulish_language" title="Gaulish language">
                 Gaulish
                </a>
               </li>
              </ul>
             </li>
             <li>
              <a href="/wiki/Insular_Celtic_languages" title="Insular Celtic languages">
               Insular Celtic
              </a>
              <ul>
               <li>
                <a href="/wiki/Brittonic_languages" title="Brittonic languages">
                 Brittonic
                </a>
               </li>
               <li>
                <a href="/wiki/Goidelic_languages" title="Goidelic languages">
                 Goidelic
                </a>
               </li>
              </ul>
             </li>
            </ul>
           </li>
          </ul>
         </td>
         <td style="text-align: left; vertical-align: top;">
          <p>
           "
           <b>
            P-Celtic hypothesis
           </b>
           "
          </p>
          <ul>
           <li>
            Proto-Celtic
            <ul>
             <li>
              <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Q-Celtic" title="Q-Celtic">
               Q-Celtic
              </a>
              <ul>
               <li>
                <a href="/wiki/Celtiberian_language" title="Celtiberian language">
                 Celtiberian
                </a>
               </li>
               <li>
                <a href="/wiki/Gallaecian_language" title="Gallaecian language">
                 Gallaecian
                </a>
               </li>
               <li>
                <a href="/wiki/Goidelic_languages" title="Goidelic languages">
                 Goidelic
                </a>
               </li>
              </ul>
             </li>
             <li>
              <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/P-Celtic" title="P-Celtic">
               P-Celtic
              </a>
              <ul>
               <li>
                <a href="/wiki/Gaulish_language" title="Gaulish language">
                 Gaulish
                </a>
               </li>
               <li>
                <a href="/wiki/Brittonic_languages" title="Brittonic languages">
                 Brittonic
                </a>
               </li>
              </ul>
             </li>
            </ul>
           </li>
          </ul>
         </td>
        </tr>
       </table>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span id="Eska_.282010.29">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Eska_(2010)">
        Eska (2010)
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Celtic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Eska (2010)">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Eska (2010)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
        <a href="#cite_note-64">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
       evaluates the evidence as supporting the following tree, based on
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shared_innovation" title="Shared innovation">
        shared innovations
       </a>
       , though it is not always clear that the innovations are not
       <a href="/wiki/Areal_feature" title="Areal feature">
        areal features
       </a>
       . It seems likely that Celtiberian split off before Cisalpine Celtic, but the evidence for this is not robust. On the other hand, the unity of Gaulish, Goidelic, and Brittonic is reasonably secure. Schumacher (2004, p. 86) had already cautiously considered this grouping to be likely genetic, based, among others, on the shared reformation of the sentence-initial, fully inflecting relative pronoun
       <i>
        *i̯os, *i̯ā, *i̯od
       </i>
       into an uninflected enclitic particle. Eska sees
       <a href="/wiki/Cisalpine_Gaulish" title="Cisalpine Gaulish">
        Cisalpine Gaulish
       </a>
       as more akin to Lepontic than to Transalpine Gaulish.
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Celtic
        <ul>
         <li>
          <a href="/wiki/Celtiberian_language" title="Celtiberian language">
           Celtiberian
          </a>
         </li>
         <li>
          <a href="/wiki/Gallaecian_language" title="Gallaecian language">
           Gallaecian
          </a>
         </li>
         <li>
          Nuclear Celtic?
          <ul>
           <li>
            Cisalpine Celtic:
            <a href="/wiki/Lepontic_language" title="Lepontic language">
             Lepontic
            </a>
            →
            <a href="/wiki/Cisalpine_Gaulish" title="Cisalpine Gaulish">
             Cisalpine Gaulish
            </a>
           </li>
           <li>
            Transalpine–Goidelic–Brittonic (secure)
            <ul>
             <li>
              <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transalpine_Gaulish" title="Transalpine Gaulish">
               Transalpine Gaulish
              </a>
              ("Transalpine Celtic")
             </li>
             <li>
              <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insular_Celtic" title="Insular Celtic">
               Insular Celtic
              </a>
              <ul>
               <li>
                <a href="/wiki/Goidelic_languages" title="Goidelic languages">
                 Goidelic
                </a>
               </li>
               <li>
                <a href="/wiki/Brittonic_languages" title="Brittonic languages">
                 Brittonic
                </a>
               </li>
              </ul>
             </li>
            </ul>
           </li>
          </ul>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Eska considers a division of Transalpine–Goidelic–Brittonic into Transalpine and Insular Celtic to be most probable because of the greater number of innovations in Insular Celtic than in P-Celtic, and because the Insular Celtic languages were probably not in great enough contact for those innovations to spread as part of a
       <a href="/wiki/Sprachbund" title="Sprachbund">
        sprachbund
       </a>
       . However, if they have another explanation (such as an SOV substratum language), then it is possible that P-Celtic is a valid clade, and the top branching would be:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Transalpine–Goidelic–Brittonic (P-Celtic hypothesis)
        <ul>
         <li>
          <a href="/wiki/Goidelic_languages" title="Goidelic languages">
           Goidelic
          </a>
         </li>
         <li>
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gallo-Brittonic" title="Gallo-Brittonic">
           Gallo-Brittonic
          </a>
          <ul>
           <li>
            <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transalpine_Gaulish" title="Transalpine Gaulish">
             Transalpine Gaulish
            </a>
            ("Transalpine Celtic")
           </li>
           <li>
            <a href="/wiki/Brittonic_languages" title="Brittonic languages">
             Brittonic
            </a>
           </li>
          </ul>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">
        Characteristics
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Celtic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Characteristics">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Although there are many differences between the individual Celtic languages, they do show many family resemblances.
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Consonant_mutation" title="Consonant mutation">
         consonant mutations
        </a>
        (Insular Celtic only)
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Inflected_preposition" title="Inflected preposition">
         inflected prepositions
        </a>
        (Insular Celtic only)
       </li>
       <li>
        two
        <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">
         grammatical genders
        </a>
        (modern Insular Celtic only; Old Irish and the Continental languages had three genders)
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
         <a href="#cite_note-65">
          [65]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
         [
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
           <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2012)">
            citation needed
           </span>
          </a>
         </i>
         ]
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        a
        <a href="/wiki/Vigesimal" title="Vigesimal">
         vigesimal
        </a>
        number system (counting by twenties)
        <ul>
         <li>
          Cornish
          <i>
           <span lang="kw" xml:lang="kw">
            hwetek ha dew ugens
           </span>
          </i>
          "fifty-six" (literally "sixteen and two twenty")
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
       <li>
        verb–subject–object (VSO) word order (probably Insular Celtic only)
       </li>
       <li>
        an interplay between the subjunctive, future, imperfect, and habitual, to the point that some tenses and moods have ousted others
       </li>
       <li>
        an impersonal or autonomous verb form serving as a
        <a href="/wiki/Passive_voice" title="Passive voice">
         passive
        </a>
        or
        <a href="/wiki/Intransitive_verb" title="Intransitive verb">
         intransitive
        </a>
        <ul>
         <li>
          Welsh
          <i>
           <span lang="cy" xml:lang="cy">
            dysgaf
           </span>
          </i>
          "I teach" vs.
          <i>
           <span lang="cy" xml:lang="cy">
            dysgir
           </span>
          </i>
          "is taught, one teaches"
         </li>
         <li>
          Irish
          <i>
           <span lang="ga" xml:lang="ga">
            múinim
           </span>
          </i>
          "I teach" vs.
          <i>
           <span lang="ga" xml:lang="ga">
            múintear
           </span>
          </i>
          "is taught, one teaches"
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
       <li>
        no
        <a href="/wiki/Infinitive" title="Infinitive">
         infinitives
        </a>
        , replaced by a quasi-nominal verb form called the verbal noun or
        <a href="/wiki/Verbnoun" title="Verbnoun">
         verbnoun
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        frequent use of vowel mutation as a morphological device, e.g. formation of plurals, verbal stems, etc.
       </li>
       <li>
        use of preverbal particles to signal either subordination or illocutionary force of the following clause
        <ul>
         <li>
          mutation-distinguished subordinators/relativisers
         </li>
         <li>
          <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_particle" title="Grammatical particle">
           particles
          </a>
          for
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Negation_(linguistics)" title="Negation (linguistics)">
           negation
          </a>
          , interrogation, and occasionally for affirmative declarations
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Infix" title="Infix">
         infixed
        </a>
        pronouns positioned between particles and verbs
       </li>
       <li>
        lack of simple verb for the
        <a href="/wiki/Imperfective_aspect" title="Imperfective aspect">
         imperfective
        </a>
        "have" process, with possession conveyed by a composite structure, usually BE + preposition
        <ul>
         <li>
          Cornish
          <i>
           <span lang="kw" xml:lang="kw">
            yma kath dhymm
           </span>
          </i>
          "I have a cat", literally "there is a cat to me"
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
       <li>
        use of
        <a href="/wiki/Periphrasis" title="Periphrasis">
         periphrastic
        </a>
        constructions to express verbal tense, voice, or
        <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_aspect" title="Grammatical aspect">
         aspectual
        </a>
        distinctions
       </li>
       <li>
        distinction by function of the two versions of BE verbs traditionally labelled substantive (or existential) and
        <a href="/wiki/Copula_(linguistics)" title="Copula (linguistics)">
         copula
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        bifurcated
        <a href="/wiki/Demonstrative" title="Demonstrative">
         demonstrative
        </a>
        structure
       </li>
       <li>
        suffixed pronominal supplements, called confirming or supplementary pronouns
       </li>
       <li>
        use of singulars or special forms of counted nouns, and use of a singulative suffix to make singular forms from plurals, where older singulars have disappeared
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Examples:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">
         Irish
        </a>
        :
        <i>
         <span lang="ga" xml:lang="ga">
          Ná bac le mac an bhacaigh is ní bhacfaidh mac an bhacaigh leat.
         </span>
        </i>
       </dd>
       <dd>
        (Literal translation) Don't bother with son the beggar's and not will-bother son the beggar's with-you.
        <ul>
         <li>
          <i>
           <span lang="ga" xml:lang="ga">
            bhacaigh
           </span>
          </i>
          is the genitive of
          <i>
           <span lang="ga" xml:lang="ga">
            bacach
           </span>
          </i>
          . The
          <i>
           <span lang="ga" xml:lang="ga">
            igh
           </span>
          </i>
          the result of
          <a href="/wiki/Affection_(linguistics)" title="Affection (linguistics)">
           affection
          </a>
          ; the
          <i>
           <span lang="ga" xml:lang="ga">
            bh
           </span>
          </i>
          is the
          <a href="/wiki/Lenition" title="Lenition">
           lenited
          </a>
          form of
          <i>
           <span lang="ga" xml:lang="ga">
            b
           </span>
          </i>
          .
         </li>
         <li>
          <i>
           <span lang="ga" xml:lang="ga">
            leat
           </span>
          </i>
          is the second person singular inflected form of the preposition
          <i>
           <span lang="ga" xml:lang="ga">
            le
           </span>
          </i>
          .
         </li>
         <li>
          The order is verb–subject–object (VSO) in the second half. Compare this to English or French (and possibly Continental Celtic) which are normally subject–verb–object in word order.
         </li>
        </ul>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">
         Welsh
        </a>
        :
        <i>
         <span lang="cy" xml:lang="cy">
          pedwar ar bymtheg a phedwar ugain
         </span>
        </i>
       </dd>
       <dd>
        (Literally) four on fifteen and four twenties
        <ul>
         <li>
          <i>
           <span lang="cy" xml:lang="cy">
            bymtheg
           </span>
          </i>
          is a mutated form of
          <i>
           <span lang="cy" xml:lang="cy">
            pymtheg
           </span>
          </i>
          , which is
          <i>
           <span lang="cy" xml:lang="cy">
            pump
           </span>
          </i>
          ("five") plus
          <i>
           <span lang="cy" xml:lang="cy">
            deg
           </span>
          </i>
          ("ten"). Likewise,
          <i>
           <span lang="cy" xml:lang="cy">
            phedwar
           </span>
          </i>
          is a mutated form of
          <i>
           <span lang="cy" xml:lang="cy">
            pedwar
           </span>
          </i>
          .
         </li>
         <li>
          The multiples of ten are
          <i>
           <span lang="cy" xml:lang="cy">
            deg, ugain, deg ar hugain, deugain, hanner cant, trigain, deg a thrigain, pedwar ugain, deg a phedwar ugain, cant
           </span>
          </i>
          .*
         </li>
        </ul>
       </dd>
      </dl>

      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Examples">
        Examples
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Celtic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Examples">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Article 1 of the Universal Declaration of Human Rights:
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        All human beings are born free and equal in dignity and rights. They are endowed with reason and conscience and should act towards one another in a spirit of brotherhood.
       </i>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">
         Irish
        </a>
        :
        <i>
         <span lang="ga" xml:lang="ga">
          Saolaítear na daoine uile saor agus comhionann ina ndínit agus ina gcearta. Tá bua an réasúin agus an choinsiasa acu agus dlíd iad féin d'iompar de mheon bráithreachas i leith a chéile.
         </span>
        </i>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Manx_language" title="Manx language">
         Manx
        </a>
        :
        <i>
         <span lang="gv" xml:lang="gv">
          Ta dagh ooilley pheiagh ruggit seyr as corrym ayns ard-cheim as kiartyn. Ren Jee feoiltaghey resoon as cooinsheanse orroo as by chair daue ymmyrkey ry cheilley myr braaraghyn.
         </span>
        </i>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic_language" title="Scottish Gaelic language">
         Scottish Gaelic
        </a>
        :
        <i>
         <span lang="gd" xml:lang="gd">
          Tha gach uile dhuine air a bhreith saor agus co-ionnan ann an urram 's ann an còirichean. Tha iad air am breith le reusan is le cogais agus mar sin bu chòir dhaibh a bhith beò nam measg fhèin ann an spiorad bràthaireil.
         </span>
        </i>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Breton_language" title="Breton language">
         Breton
        </a>
        :
        <i>
         <span lang="br" xml:lang="br">
          Dieub ha par en o dellezegezh hag o gwirioù eo ganet an holl dud. Poell ha skiant zo dezho ha dleout a reont bevañ an eil gant egile en ur spered a genvreudeuriezh.
         </span>
        </i>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Cornish_language" title="Cornish language">
         Cornish
        </a>
        :
        <i>
         <span lang="kw" xml:lang="kw">
          Genys frank ha par yw oll tus an bys yn aga dynita hag yn aga gwiryow. Enduys yns gans reson ha kowses hag y tal dhedha omdhon an eyl orth y gila yn spyrys a vrederedh.
         </span>
        </i>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">
         Welsh
        </a>
        :
        <i>
         <span lang="cy" xml:lang="cy">
          Genir pawb yn rhydd ac yn gydradd â'i gilydd mewn urddas a hawliau. Fe'u cynysgaeddir â rheswm a chydwybod, a dylai pawb ymddwyn y naill at y llall mewn ysbryd cymodlon.
         </span>
        </i>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Possibly_Celtic_languages">
        Possibly Celtic languages
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Celtic_languages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Possibly Celtic languages">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       It has been suggested that several poorly-documented languages may possibly have been Celtic.
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <b>
         <a href="/wiki/Camunic_language" title="Camunic language">
          Camunic
         </a>
        </b>
        is an extinct language which was spoken in the first millennium
        <a href="/wiki/Anno_Domini" title="Anno Domini">
         BC
        </a>
        in the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Valcamonica" title="Valcamonica">
         Valcamonica
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Valtellina" title="Valtellina">
         Valtellina
        </a>
        valleys of the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Central_Alps" title="Central Alps">
         Central Alps
        </a>
        . It has most recently been proposed to be a Celtic language.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
         <a href="#cite_note-66">
          [66]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         <a href="/wiki/Ligurian_language_(ancient)" title="Ligurian language (ancient)">
          Ligurian
         </a>
        </b>
        was spoken in the Northern Mediterranean Coast straddling the southeast French and northwest Italian coasts, including parts of
        <a href="/wiki/Tuscany" title="Tuscany">
         Tuscany
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Elba" title="Elba">
         Elba
        </a>
        island and
        <a href="/wiki/Corsica" title="Corsica">
         Corsica
        </a>
        . Xavier Delamarre argues that Ligurian was a Celtic language, similar to, but not the same as Gaulish.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
         <a href="#cite_note-67">
          [67]
         </a>
        </sup>
        The Ligurian-Celtic question is also discussed by Barruol (1999). Ancient Ligurian is either listed as Celtic (epigraphic),
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kruta1_68-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-kruta1-68">
          [68]
         </a>
        </sup>
        or Para-Celtic (onomastic).
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kruta2_48-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-kruta2-48">
          [48]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         <a href="/wiki/Lusitanian_language" title="Lusitanian language">
          Lusitanian
         </a>
        </b>
        was spoken in the area between the Douro and Tagus rivers of western
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iberia" title="Iberia">
         Iberia
        </a>
        (a region straddling the present border of
        <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">
         Portugal
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">
         Spain
        </a>
        ). It is known from only five inscriptions and various place names.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wodtko_69-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Wodtko-69">
          [69]
         </a>
        </sup>
        It is an Indo-European language and some scholars have proposed that it may be a para-Celtic language, which evolved alongside Celtic or formed a
        <a href="/wiki/Dialect_continuum" title="Dialect continuum">
         dialect continuum
        </a>
        or
        <a href="/wiki/Sprachbund" title="Sprachbund">
         sprachbund
        </a>
        with Tartessian and Gallaecian. This is tied to a theory of an Iberian origin for the Celtic languages.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wodtko_69-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Wodtko-69">
          [69]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cunliffe_70-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-cunliffe-70">
          [70]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
         <a href="#cite_note-71">
          [71]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        It is also possible that the Q-Celtic languages alone, including Goidelic, originated in western Iberia (a theory that was first put forward by
        <a href="/wiki/Edward_Lhuyd" title="Edward Lhuyd">
         Edward Lhuyd
        </a>
        in 1707) or shared a common linguistic ancestor with Lusitanian.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
         <a href="#cite_note-72">
          [72]
         </a>
        </sup>
        Secondary evidence for this hypothesis has been found in research by biological scientists, who have identified (firstly) deep-rooted similarities in human DNA found precisely in both the former
        <a href="/wiki/Lusitania" title="Lusitania">
         Lusitania
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">
         Ireland
        </a>
        ,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
         <a href="#cite_note-73">
          [73]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
         <a href="#cite_note-74">
          [74]
         </a>
        </sup>
        and; (secondly) the so-called "
        <a href="/wiki/Disjunct_distribution#Lusitanian_distribution" title="Disjunct distribution">
         Lusitanian distribution
        </a>
        " of animals and plants unique to western Iberia and Ireland. Both of these phenomena are now generally believed to have resulted from human emigration from Iberia to Ireland, during the late
        <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic" title="Paleolithic">
         Paleolithic
        </a>
        or early
        <a href="/wiki/Mesolithic" title="Mesolithic">
         Mesolithic
        </a>
        eras.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mascheretti_et_al._(2003)_75-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Mascheretti_et_al._(2003)-75">
          [75]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </dd>
       <dd>
        Other scholars see greater linguistic affinities between Lusitanian,
        <a href="/wiki/Italic_languages" title="Italic languages">
         proto-Italic
        </a>
        and
        <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Old_European_language&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Old European language (page does not exist)">
         Old European
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Villar2000_76-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Villar2000-76">
          [76]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
         <a href="#cite_note-77">
          [77]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <b>
         <a href="/wiki/Pictish_language" title="Pictish language">
          Pictish
         </a>
        </b>
        was for a long time thought to be a
        <a href="/wiki/Pre-Indo-European_languages" title="Pre-Indo-European languages">
         pre-Celtic, non-Indo-European language
        </a>
        of Scotland. Some believe it was an
        <a href="/wiki/Insular_Celtic_languages" title="Insular Celtic languages">
         Insular Celtic language
        </a>
        allied to the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/P-Celtic_and_Q-Celtic_languages" title="P-Celtic and Q-Celtic languages">
         P-Celtic
        </a>
        language
        <a href="/wiki/Common_Brittonic" title="Common Brittonic">
         Brittonic
        </a>
        (descendants
        <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">
         Welsh
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Cornish_language" title="Cornish language">
         Cornish
        </a>
        ,
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cumbric_language" title="Cumbric language">
         Cumbric
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Breton_language" title="Breton language">
         Breton
        </a>
        ).
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FFFW_78-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-FFFW-78">
          [78]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         <a href="/wiki/Rhaetian_language" title="Rhaetian language">
          Rhaetian
         </a>
        </b>
        was spoken in central parts of present-day
        <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">
         Switzerland
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Tyrol_(state)" title="Tyrol (state)">
         Tyrol
        </a>
        in
        <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">
         Austria
        </a>
        , and the Alpine regions of northeastern
        <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">
         Italy
        </a>
        . It is documented by a limited number of short inscriptions (found through Northern Italy and Western Austria) in two variants of the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Etruscan_alphabet" title="Etruscan alphabet">
         Etruscan alphabet
        </a>
        . Its linguistic categorization is not clearly established, and it presents a confusing mixture of what appear to be
        <a href="/wiki/Etruscan_language" title="Etruscan language">
         Etruscan
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">
         Indo-European
        </a>
        , and uncertain other elements.
        <a href="/wiki/Howard_Hayes_Scullard" title="Howard Hayes Scullard">
         Howard Hayes Scullard
        </a>
        argues that Rhaetian was also a Celtic language.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
         <a href="#cite_note-79">
          [79]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <b>
         <a href="/wiki/Tartessian_language" title="Tartessian language">
          Tartessian
         </a>
        </b>
        , spoken in the southwest of the Iberia Peninsula (mainly southern
        <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">
         Portugal
        </a>
        and southwestern
        <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">
         Spain
        </a>
        ).
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-koch2_80-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-koch2-80">
          [80]
         </a>
        </sup>
        Tartessian is known by 95 inscriptions, with the longest having 82 readable signs.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cunliffe_70-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-cunliffe-70">
          [70]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Colera_81-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Colera-81">
          [81]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-koch2011_82-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-koch2011-82">
          [82]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <a href="/wiki/John_T._Koch" title="John T. Koch">
         John T. Koch
        </a>
        argues that Tartessian was also a Celtic language.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-koch2011_82-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-koch2011-82">
          [82]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>

          <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=f899xH_quaMC&amp;lpg=PA233&amp;ots=p-RudfBy1H&amp;dq=botorrita%20ii&amp;pg=PA233#v=onepage&amp;q=botorrita%20ii&amp;f=false" rel="nofollow">
           Celtic Culture: A Historical Encyclopedia
          </a>
          John T. Koch, Vol 1, p. 233
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-52">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-52">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          <cite class="citation book">
           Prósper, B.M. (2002).
           <i>
            Lenguas y religiones prerromanas del occidente de la península ibérica
           </i>
           . Ediciones Universidad de Salamanca. pp. 422–27.
           <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">
            ISBN
           </a>
           <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/84-7800-818-7" title="Special:BookSources/84-7800-818-7">
            84-7800-818-7
           </a>
           .
          </cite>
          <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Lenguas+y+religiones+prerromanas+del+occidente+de+la+pen%C3%ADnsula+ib%C3%A9rica&amp;rft.pages=422-27&amp;rft.pub=Ediciones+Universidad+de+Salamanca&amp;rft.date=2002&amp;rft.isbn=84-7800-818-7&amp;rft.aulast=Pr%C3%B3sper&amp;rft.aufirst=B.M.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACeltic+languages">
           <span style="display:none;">
           </span>
          </span>
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-53">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-53">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          Villar F., B. M. Prósper. (2005).
          <i>
           Vascos, Celtas e Indoeuropeos: genes y lenguas. Ediciones Universidad de Salamanca
          </i>
          . pgs. 333–350.
          <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">
           ISBN
          </a>
          <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/84-7800-530-7" title="Special:BookSources/84-7800-530-7">
           84-7800-530-7
          </a>
          .
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-54">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-54">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          "In the northwest of the Iberian Peninula, and more specifically between the west and north Atlantic coasts and an imaginary line running north-south and linking Oviedo and Merida, there is a corpus of Latin inscriptions with particular characteristics of its own. This corpus contains some linguistic features that are clearly Celtic and others that in our opinion are not Celtic. The former we shall group, for the moment, under the label northwestern Hispano-Celtic. The latter are the same features found in well-documented contemporary inscriptions in the region occupied by the Lusitanians, and therefore belonging to the variety known as LUSITANIAN, or more broadly as GALLO-LUSITANIAN. As we have already said, we do not consider this variety to belong to the Celtic language family." Jordán Colera 2007: p.750
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-55">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-55">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_H._Jackson" title="Kenneth H. Jackson">
           Kenneth H. Jackson
          </a>
          suggested that there were two Pictish languages, a pre-Indo-European one and a
          <i>
           <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pritennic" title="Pritennic">
            Pritenic
           </a>
          </i>
          Celtic one. This has been challenged by some scholars. See
          <a href="/wiki/Katherine_Forsyth" title="Katherine Forsyth">
           Katherine Forsyth
          </a>
          's
          <i>
           "Language in Pictland: the case against 'non-Indo-European Pictish'"
          </i>
          <cite class="citation web">
           <a class="external text" href="http://eprints.gla.ac.uk/archive/00002081/01/languagepictland.pdf" rel="nofollow">
            "Etext"
           </a>
           <span style="font-size:85%;">
            (PDF)
           </span>
           .
          </cite>
          <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Etext&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Feprints.gla.ac.uk%2Farchive%2F00002081%2F01%2Flanguagepictland.pdf&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACeltic+languages">
           <span style="display:none;">
           </span>
          </span>
          <small>
           (27.8
           <a href="/wiki/Megabyte" title="Megabyte">
            MB
           </a>
           )
          </small>
          . See also the introduction by James &amp; Taylor to the
          <i>

          </i>

           <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20060220054951/http://www.st-andrews.ac.uk/institutes/sassi/spns/INDEX2INTRO.pdf" rel="nofollow">

           </a>

            (PDF)
           </span>
           on 20 February 2006.

        <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Die+keltischen+Prim%C3%A4rverben.+Ein+vergleichendes%2C+etymologisches+und+morphologisches+Lexikon&amp;rft.place=Innsbruck&amp;rft.pub=Institut+f%C3%BCr+Sprachen+und+Kulturen+der+Universit%C3%A4t+Innsbruck&amp;rft.date=2004&amp;rft.isbn=3-85124-692-6&amp;rft.aulast=Schumacher&amp;rft.aufirst=Stefan&amp;rft.au=Schulze-Thulin%2C+Britta&amp;rft.au=aan+de+Wiel%2C+Caroline&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACeltic+languages">
         <span style="display:none;">
         </span>
        </span>
       </li>
      </ul>
